:   []{#iref-bodystructure-fetch-item-10 .iref} The
    \[[MIME-IMB](#RFC2045){.xref}\] body structure of the message. This
    is computed by the server by parsing the
    \[[MIME-IMB](#RFC2045){.xref}\] header fields in the
    \[[RFC5322](#RFC5322){.xref}\] header and
    \[[MIME-IMB](#RFC2045){.xref}\] headers. See [Section
    7.5.2](#fetch-response){.xref} for more
    details.[¶](#section-6.4.5-9.14){.pilcrow}
:   

ENVELOPE
:   []{#iref-envelope-fetch-item-110 .iref} The envelope structure of
    the message. This is computed by the server by parsing the
    \[[RFC5322](#RFC5322){.xref}\] header into the component parts,
    defaulting various fields as necessary. See [Section
    7.5.2](#fetch-response){.xref} for more
    details.[¶](#section-6.4.5-9.16){.pilcrow}
:   

FLAGS
:   []{#iref-flags-fetch-item-111 .iref} The flags that are set for this
    message.[¶](#section-6.4.5-9.18){.pilcrow}
:   

INTERNALDATE
:   []{#iref-internaldate-fetch-item-112 .iref} The internal date of the
    message.[¶](#section-6.4.5-9.20){.pilcrow}
:   

RFC822.SIZE
:   []{#iref-rfc822size-fetch-item-113 .iref} The size of the message,
    as defined in [Section
    2.3.4](#RFC822.SIZE_message_attribute){.xref}.[¶](#section-6.4.5-9.22){.pilcrow}
:   

UID
:   []{#iref-uid-fetch-item-114 .iref} The unique identifier for the
    message.[¶](#section-6.4.5-9.24){.pilcrow}
:   

Example:[¶](#section-6.4.5-10){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-6.4.5-11}
``` sourcecode
  C: A654 FETCH 2:4 (FLAGS BODY[HEADER.FIELDS (DATE FROM)])
  S: * 2 FETCH ....
  S: * 3 FETCH ....
  S: * 4 FETCH ....
  S: A654 OK FETCH completed
```

[¶](#section-6.4.5-11){.pilcrow}
:::

::: {#fetch-section}
::: {#section-6.4.5.1 .section}
##### [6.4.5.1.](#section-6.4.5.1){.section-number .selfRef} [FETCH Section Specification](#name-fetch-section-specification){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-fetch-section-specification}

Several FETCH data items reference \"section\" or \"section-binary\".
The section specification is a set of zero or more part specifiers
delimited by periods. A part specifier is either a part number or one of
the following: HEADER, HEADER.FIELDS, HEADER.FIELDS.NOT, MIME, and TEXT.
(Non-numeric part specifiers have to be the last specifier in a section
specification.) An empty section specification refers to the entire
message, including the header.[¶](#section-6.4.5.1-1){.pilcrow}

Every message has at least one part number. Messages that do not use
MIME, and MIME messages that are not multipart and have no encapsulated
message within them, only have a part
1.[¶](#section-6.4.5.1-2){.pilcrow}

Multipart messages are assigned consecutive part numbers, as they occur
in the message. If a particular part is of type message or multipart,
its parts [MUST]{.bcp14} be indicated by a period followed by the part
number within that nested multipart
part.[¶](#section-6.4.5.1-3){.pilcrow}

A part of type MESSAGE/RFC822 or MESSAGE/GLOBAL also has nested part
numbers, referring to parts of the MESSAGE part\'s
body.[¶](#section-6.4.5.1-4){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-header-part-specifier-115 .iref}
[]{#iref-headerfields-part-specifier .iref}
[]{#iref-headerfieldsnot-part-specif .iref}
[]{#iref-text-part-specifier-118 .iref} The HEADER, HEADER.FIELDS,
HEADER.FIELDS.NOT, and TEXT part specifiers can be the sole part
specifier or can be prefixed by one or more numeric part specifiers,
provided that the numeric part specifier refers to a part of type
MESSAGE/RFC822 or MESSAGE/GLOBAL. The MIME part specifier [MUST]{.bcp14}
be prefixed by one or more numeric part
specifiers.[¶](#section-6.4.5.1-5){.pilcrow}

The HEADER, HEADER.FIELDS, and HEADER.FIELDS.NOT part specifiers refer
to the \[[RFC5322](#RFC5322){.xref}\] header of the message or of an
encapsulated \[[MIME-IMT](#RFC2046){.xref}\] MESSAGE/RFC822 or
MESSAGE/GLOBAL message. HEADER.FIELDS and HEADER.FIELDS.NOT are followed
by a list of field-names (as defined in \[[RFC5322](#RFC5322){.xref}\])
and return a subset of the header. The subset returned by HEADER.FIELDS
contains only those header fields with a field-name that matches one of
the names in the list; similarly, the subset returned by
HEADER.FIELDS.NOT contains only the header fields with a non-matching
field-name. The field-matching is ASCII-range case insensitive but is
otherwise exact. Subsetting does not exclude the
\[[RFC5322](#RFC5322){.xref}\] delimiting blank line between the header
and the body; the blank line is included in all header fetches, except
in the case of a message that has no body and no blank
line.[¶](#section-6.4.5.1-6){.pilcrow}

[]{#iref-mime-part-specifier-119 .iref} The MIME part specifier refers
to the \[[MIME-IMB](#RFC2045){.xref}\] header for this
part.[¶](#section-6.4.5.1-7){.pilcrow}

The TEXT part specifier refers to the text body of the message, omitting
the \[[RFC5322](#RFC5322){.xref}\]
header.[¶](#section-6.4.5.1-8){.pilcrow}

Here is an example of a complex message with some of its part
specifiers:[¶](#section-6.4.5.1-9){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-6.4.5.1-10 .artwork .art-text .alignLeft}
      HEADER     ([RFC5322] header of the message)
      TEXT       ([RFC5322] text body of the message) MULTIPART/MIXED
      1          TEXT/PLAIN
      2          APPLICATION/OCTET-STREAM
      3          MESSAGE/RFC822
      3.HEADER   ([RFC5322] header of the message)
      3.TEXT     ([RFC5322] text body of the message) MULTIPART/MIXED
      3.1        TEXT/PLAIN
      3.2        APPLICATION/OCTET-STREAM
      4          MULTIPART/MIXED
      4.1        IMAGE/GIF
      4.1.MIME   ([MIME-IMB] header for the IMAGE/GIF)
      4.2        MESSAGE/RFC822
      4.2.HEADER ([RFC5322] header of the message)
      4.2.TEXT   ([RFC5322] text body of the message) MULTIPART/MIXED
      4.2.1      TEXT/PLAIN
      4.2.2      MULTIPART/ALTERNATIVE
      4.2.2.1    TEXT/PLAIN
      4.2.2.2    TEXT/RICHTEXT

[¶](#section-6.4.5.1-10){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#section-6.4.6 .section}
#### [6.4.6.](#section-6.4.6){.section-number .selfRef} [STORE Command](#name-store-command){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-store-command}

[]{#iref-store-command-120 .iref} []{.break}

Arguments:

:   sequence set[¶](#section-6.4.6-1.2.1){.pilcrow}

    message data item name[¶](#section-6.4.6-1.2.2){.pilcrow}

    value for message data item[¶](#section-6.4.6-1.2.3){.pilcrow}

:   

Responses:
:   []{.break}

    untagged responses:
    :   FETCH[¶](#section-6.4.6-1.4.1.2){.pilcrow}
    :   
:   

Result:
:   []{.break}

    OK -
    :   store completed[¶](#section-6.4.6-1.6.1.2){.pilcrow}
    :   

    NO -
    :   store error: can\'t store that
        data[¶](#section-6.4.6-1.6.1.4){.pilcrow}
    :   

    BAD -
    :   command unknown or arguments
        invalid[¶](#section-6.4.6-1.6.1.6){.pilcrow}
    :   
:   

The STORE command alters data associated with a message in the mailbox.
Normally, STORE will return the updated value of the data with an
untagged FETCH response. A suffix of \".SILENT\" in the data item name
prevents the untagged FETCH, and the server [SHOULD]{.bcp14} assume that
the client has determined the updated value itself or does not care
about the updated value.[¶](#section-6.4.6-2){.pilcrow}

Note: Regardless of whether or not the \".SILENT\" suffix was used, the
server [SHOULD]{.bcp14} send an untagged FETCH response if a change to a
message\'s flags from an external source is observed. The intent is that
the status of the flags is determinate without a race
condition.[¶](#section-6.4.6-3){.pilcrow}

The currently defined data items that can be stored
are:[¶](#section-6.4.6-4){.pilcrow}

[]{.break}

FLAGS \<flag list>
:   []{#iref-flags-flag-list-store-comma .iref} Replace the flags for
    the message with the argument. The new value of the flags is
    returned as if a FETCH of those flags was
    done.[¶](#section-6.4.6-5.2){.pilcrow}
:   

FLAGS.SILENT \<flag list>
:   []{#iref-flagssilent-flag-list-store .iref} Equivalent to FLAGS, but
    without returning a new value.[¶](#section-6.4.6-5.4){.pilcrow}
:   

+FLAGS \<flag list>
:   []{#iref-flags-flag-list-123 .iref} Add the argument to the flags
    for the message. The new value of the flags is returned as if a
    FETCH of those flags was done.[¶](#section-6.4.6-5.6){.pilcrow}
:   

+FLAGS.SILENT \<flag list>
:   []{#iref-flagssilent-flag-list-124 .iref} Equivalent to +FLAGS, but
    without returning a new value.[¶](#section-6.4.6-5.8){.pilcrow}
:   

-FLAGS \<flag list>
:   []{#iref-flags-flag-list-125 .iref} Remove the argument from the
    flags for the message. The new value of the flags is returned as if
    a FETCH of those flags was done.[¶](#section-6.4.6-5.10){.pilcrow}
:   

-FLAGS.SILENT \<flag list>
:   []{#iref-flagssilent-flag-list-126 .iref} Equivalent to -FLAGS, but
    without returning a new value.[¶](#section-6.4.6-5.12){.pilcrow}
:   

Example:[¶](#section-6.4.6-6){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-6.4.6-7}
``` sourcecode
  C: A003 STORE 2:4 +FLAGS (\Deleted)
  S: * 2 FETCH (FLAGS (\Deleted \Seen))
  S: * 3 FETCH (FLAGS (\Deleted))
  S: * 4 FETCH (FLAGS (\Deleted \Flagged \Seen))
  S: A003 OK STORE completed
```

[¶](#section-6.4.6-7){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#copy-command}
::: {#section-6.4.7 .section}
#### [6.4.7.](#section-6.4.7){.section-number .selfRef} [COPY Command](#name-copy-command){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-copy-command}

[]{#iref-copy-command-127 .iref} []{.break}

Arguments:

:   sequence set[¶](#section-6.4.7-1.2.1){.pilcrow}

    mailbox name[¶](#section-6.4.7-1.2.2){.pilcrow}

:   

Responses:
:   no specific responses for this
    command[¶](#section-6.4.7-1.4){.pilcrow}
:   

Result:
:   []{.break}

    OK -
    :   copy completed[¶](#section-6.4.7-1.6.1.2){.pilcrow}
    :   

    NO -
    :   copy error: can\'t copy those messages or to that
        name[¶](#section-6.4.7-1.6.1.4){.pilcrow}
    :   

    BAD -
    :   command unknown or arguments
        invalid[¶](#section-6.4.7-1.6.1.6){.pilcrow}
    :   
:   

The COPY command copies the specified message(s) to the end of the
specified destination mailbox. The flags and internal date of the
message(s) [SHOULD]{.bcp14} be preserved in the
copy.[¶](#section-6.4.7-2){.pilcrow}

If the destination mailbox does not exist, a server [MUST]{.bcp14}
return an error. It [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} automatically create the mailbox.
Unless it is certain that the destination mailbox can not be created,
the server [MUST]{.bcp14} send the response code \"\[TRYCREATE\]\" as
the prefix of the text of the tagged NO response. This gives a hint to
the client that it can attempt a CREATE command and retry the COPY if
the CREATE is successful.[¶](#section-6.4.7-3){.pilcrow}

If the COPY command is unsuccessful for any reason, server
implementations [MUST]{.bcp14} restore the destination mailbox to its
state before the COPY attempt (other than possibly incrementing
UIDNEXT), i.e., partial copy [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} be
done.[¶](#section-6.4.7-4){.pilcrow}

On successful completion of a COPY, the server returns a COPYUID
response code (see [Section 7.1](#server-status-responses){.xref}). Two
exceptions to this requirement are listed
below.[¶](#section-6.4.7-5){.pilcrow}

In the case of a mailbox that has permissions set so that the client can
COPY to the mailbox, but not SELECT or EXAMINE it, the server [MUST
NOT]{.bcp14} send a COPYUID response code as it would disclose
information about the mailbox.[¶](#section-6.4.7-6){.pilcrow}

In the case of a mailbox that has UIDNOTSTICKY status (see [Section
7.1](#server-status-responses){.xref}), the server [MAY]{.bcp14} omit
the COPYUID response code as it is not
meaningful.[¶](#section-6.4.7-7){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-6.4.7-8){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-6.4.7-9}
``` sourcecode
  C: A003 COPY 2:4 MEETING
  S: A003 OK [COPYUID 38505 304,319:320 3956:3958] COPY completed
```

[¶](#section-6.4.7-9){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::

::: {#section-6.4.8 .section}
#### [6.4.8.](#section-6.4.8){.section-number .selfRef} [MOVE Command](#name-move-command){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-move-command}

[]{#iref-move-command-128 .iref} []{.break}

Arguments:

:   sequence set[¶](#section-6.4.8-1.2.1){.pilcrow}

    mailbox name[¶](#section-6.4.8-1.2.2){.pilcrow}

:   

Responses:
:   no specific responses for this
    command[¶](#section-6.4.8-1.4){.pilcrow}
:   

Result:
:   []{.break}

    OK -
    :   move completed[¶](#section-6.4.8-1.6.1.2){.pilcrow}
    :   

    NO -
    :   move error: can\'t move those messages or to that
        name[¶](#section-6.4.8-1.6.1.4){.pilcrow}
    :   

    BAD -
    :   command unknown or arguments
        invalid[¶](#section-6.4.8-1.6.1.6){.pilcrow}
    :   
:   

The MOVE command moves the specified message(s) to the end of the
specified destination mailbox. The flags and internal date of the
message(s) [SHOULD]{.bcp14} be preserved.[¶](#section-6.4.8-2){.pilcrow}

This means that a new message is created in the target mailbox with a
new UID, the original message is removed from the source mailbox, and it
appears to the client as a single action. This has the same effect for
each message as this sequence:[¶](#section-6.4.8-3){.pilcrow}

1.  [\[UID\] COPY[¶](#section-6.4.8-4.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-6.4.8-4.1}
2.  [\[UID\] STORE +FLAGS.SILENT
    \\DELETED[¶](#section-6.4.8-4.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-6.4.8-4.2}
3.  [UID EXPUNGE[¶](#section-6.4.8-4.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-6.4.8-4.3}

Although the effect of the MOVE is the same as the preceding steps, the
semantics are not identical: the intermediate states produced by those
steps do not occur, and the response codes are different. In particular,
though the COPY and EXPUNGE response codes will be returned, response
codes for a STORE [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} be generated, and the \\Deleted
flag [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} be set for any
message.[¶](#section-6.4.8-5){.pilcrow}

Unlike the COPY command, MOVE of a set of messages might fail partway
through the set. Regardless of whether the command is successful in
moving the entire set, each individual message [MUST]{.bcp14} be either
moved or unaffected. The server [MUST]{.bcp14} leave each message in a
state where it is in at least one of the source or target mailboxes (no
message can be lost or orphaned). The server [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} leave
any message in both mailboxes (it would be bad for a partial failure to
result in a bunch of duplicate messages). This is true even if the
server returns a tagged NO response to the
command.[¶](#section-6.4.8-6){.pilcrow}

If the destination mailbox does not exist, a server [MUST]{.bcp14}
return an error. It [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} automatically create the mailbox.
Unless it is certain that the destination mailbox cannot be created, the
server [MUST]{.bcp14} send the response code \"\[TRYCREATE\]\" as the
prefix of the text of the tagged NO response. This gives a hint to the
client that it can attempt a CREATE command and retry the MOVE if the
CREATE is successful.[¶](#section-6.4.8-7){.pilcrow}

Because of the similarity of MOVE to COPY, extensions that affect COPY
affect MOVE in the same way. Response codes listed in [Section
7.1](#server-status-responses){.xref}, as well as those defined by
extensions, are sent as indicated for
COPY.[¶](#section-6.4.8-8){.pilcrow}

Servers send COPYUID in response to a MOVE or a UID MOVE (see [Section
6.4.9](#uid-commands){.xref}) command. For additional information about
COPYUID, see [Section 7.1](#server-status-responses){.xref}. Note that
there are several exceptions listed in [Section
6.4.7](#copy-command){.xref} that allow servers not to return
COPYUID.[¶](#section-6.4.8-9){.pilcrow}

Servers are also [REQUIRED]{.bcp14} to send the COPYUID response code in
an untagged OK before sending EXPUNGE or similar responses. (Sending
COPYUID in the tagged OK, as described in [Section
6.4.7](#copy-command){.xref}, means that clients first receive an
EXPUNGE for a message and afterwards COPYUID for the same message. It
can be unnecessarily difficult to process that sequence
usefully.)[¶](#section-6.4.8-10){.pilcrow}

An example:[¶](#section-6.4.8-11){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-6.4.8-12}
``` sourcecode
  C: a UID MOVE 42:69 foo
  S: * OK [COPYUID 432432 42:69 1202:1229]
  S: * 22 EXPUNGE
  ...More EXPUNGE responses from the server...
  S: a OK Done
```

[¶](#section-6.4.8-12){.pilcrow}
:::

Note that the server may send unrelated EXPUNGE responses as well, if
any happen to have been expunged at the same time; this is normal IMAP
operation.[¶](#section-6.4.8-13){.pilcrow}

Note that moving a message to the currently selected mailbox (that is,
where the source and target mailboxes are the same) is allowed when
copying the message to the currently selected mailbox is
allowed.[¶](#section-6.4.8-14){.pilcrow}

The server may send EXPUNGE responses before the tagged response, so the
client cannot safely send more commands with message sequence number
arguments while the server is processing
MOVE.[¶](#section-6.4.8-15){.pilcrow}

MOVE and UID MOVE can be pipelined with other commands, but care has to
be taken. Both commands modify sequence numbers and also allow unrelated
EXPUNGE responses. The renumbering of other messages in the source
mailbox following any EXPUNGE response can be surprising and makes it
unsafe to pipeline any command that relies on message sequence numbers
after a MOVE or UID MOVE. Similarly, MOVE cannot be pipelined with a
command that might cause message renumbering. See [Section
5.5](#pipelining){.xref} for more information about ambiguities as well
as handling requirements for both clients and
servers.[¶](#section-6.4.8-16){.pilcrow}
:::

::: {#uid-commands}
::: {#section-6.4.9 .section}
#### [6.4.9.](#section-6.4.9){.section-number .selfRef} [UID Command](#name-uid-command){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-uid-command}

[]{#iref-uid-command-129 .iref} []{.break}

Arguments:

:   command name[¶](#section-6.4.9-1.2.1){.pilcrow}

    command arguments[¶](#section-6.4.9-1.2.2){.pilcrow}

:   

Responses:
:   []{.break}

    untagged responses:
    :   FETCH, ESEARCH, EXPUNGE[¶](#section-6.4.9-1.4.1.2){.pilcrow}
    :   
:   

Result:
:   []{.break}

    OK -
    :   UID command completed[¶](#section-6.4.9-1.6.1.2){.pilcrow}
    :   

    NO -
    :   UID command error[¶](#section-6.4.9-1.6.1.4){.pilcrow}
    :   

    BAD -
    :   command unknown or arguments
        invalid[¶](#section-6.4.9-1.6.1.6){.pilcrow}
    :   
:   

The UID command has three forms. In the first form, it takes as its
arguments a COPY, MOVE, FETCH, or STORE command with arguments
appropriate for the associated command. However, the numbers in the
sequence-set argument are unique identifiers instead of message sequence
numbers. Sequence-set ranges are permitted, but there is no guarantee
that unique identifiers will be
contiguous.[¶](#section-6.4.9-2){.pilcrow}

A non-existent unique identifier is ignored without any error message
generated. Thus, it is possible for a UID FETCH command to return an OK
without any data or a UID COPY, UID MOVE, or UID STORE to return an OK
without performing any operations.[¶](#section-6.4.9-3){.pilcrow}

In the second form, the UID command takes an EXPUNGE command with an
extra parameter that specifies a sequence set of UIDs to operate on. The
UID EXPUNGE command permanently removes all messages that have both the
\\Deleted flag set and a UID that is included in the specified sequence
set from the currently selected mailbox. If a message either does not
have the \\Deleted flag set or has a UID that is not included in the
specified sequence set, it is not
affected.[¶](#section-6.4.9-4){.pilcrow}

UID EXPUNGE is particularly useful for disconnected use clients. By
using UID EXPUNGE instead of EXPUNGE when resynchronizing with the
server, the client can ensure that it does not inadvertently remove any
messages that have been marked as \\Deleted by other clients between the
time that the client was last connected and the time the client
resynchronizes.[¶](#section-6.4.9-5){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-6.4.9-6){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-6.4.9-7}
``` sourcecode
  C: A003 UID EXPUNGE 3000:3002
  S: * 3 EXPUNGE
  S: * 3 EXPUNGE
  S: * 3 EXPUNGE
  S: A003 OK UID EXPUNGE completed
```

[¶](#section-6.4.9-7){.pilcrow}
:::

In the third form, the UID command takes a SEARCH command with SEARCH
command arguments. The interpretation of the arguments is the same as
with SEARCH; however, the numbers returned in an ESEARCH response for a
UID SEARCH command are unique identifiers instead of message sequence
numbers. Also, the corresponding ESEARCH response [MUST]{.bcp14} include
the UID indicator. For example, the command UID SEARCH 1:100 UID 443:557
returns the unique identifiers corresponding to the intersection of two
sequence sets, the message sequence number range 1:100, and the UID
range 443:557.[¶](#section-6.4.9-8){.pilcrow}

Note: in the above example, the UID range 443:557 appears. The same
comment about a non-existent unique identifier being ignored without any
error message also applies here. Hence, even if neither UID 443 or 557
exist, this range is valid and would include an existing UID
495.[¶](#section-6.4.9-9){.pilcrow}

Also note that a UID range of 559:\* always includes the UID of the last
message in the mailbox, even if 559 is higher than any assigned UID
value. This is because the contents of a range are independent of the
order of the range endpoints. Thus, any UID range with \* as one of the
endpoints indicates at least one message (the message with the highest
numbered UID), unless the mailbox is
empty.[¶](#section-6.4.9-10){.pilcrow}

The number after the \"\*\" in an untagged FETCH or EXPUNGE response is
always a message sequence number, not a unique identifier, even for a
UID command response. However, server implementations [MUST]{.bcp14}
implicitly include the UID message data item as part of any FETCH
response caused by a UID command, regardless of whether a UID was
specified as a message data item to the
FETCH.[¶](#section-6.4.9-11){.pilcrow}

Note: The rule about including the UID message data item as part of a
FETCH response primarily applies to the UID FETCH and UID STORE
commands, including a UID FETCH command that does not include UID as a
message data item. Although it is unlikely that the other UID commands
will cause an untagged FETCH, this rule applies to these commands as
well.[¶](#section-6.4.9-12){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-6.4.9-13){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-6.4.9-14}
``` sourcecode
  C: A999 UID FETCH 4827313:4828442 FLAGS
  S: * 23 FETCH (FLAGS (\Seen) UID 4827313)
  S: * 24 FETCH (FLAGS (\Seen) UID 4827943)
  S: * 25 FETCH (FLAGS (\Seen) UID 4828442)
  S: A999 OK UID FETCH completed
```

[¶](#section-6.4.9-14){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#section-6.5 .section}
### [6.5.](#section-6.5){.section-number .selfRef} [Client Commands - Experimental/Expansion](#name-client-commands-experimenta){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-client-commands-experimenta}

Each command that is not part of this specification [MUST]{.bcp14} have
at least one capability name (see [Section
6.1.1](#capability-command){.xref}) associated with it. (Multiple
commands can be associated with the same capability
name.)[¶](#section-6.5-1){.pilcrow}

Server implementations [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send any added untagged
responses (not specified in this specification), unless the client
requested it by issuing the associated experimental command (specified
in an extension document) or the ENABLE command ([Section
6.3.1](#enable-command){.xref}).[¶](#section-6.5-2){.pilcrow}

The following example demonstrates how a client can check for the
presence of a fictitious XPIG-LATIN capability that adds the XPIG-LATIN
command and the XPIG-LATIN untagged response. (Note that for an
extension, the command name and the capability name don\'t have to be
the same.)[¶](#section-6.5-3){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-6.5-4){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-6.5-5}
``` sourcecode
  C: a441 CAPABILITY
  S: * CAPABILITY IMAP4rev2 XPIG-LATIN
  S: a441 OK CAPABILITY completed
  C: A442 XPIG-LATIN
  S: * XPIG-LATIN ow-nay eaking-spay ig-pay atin-lay
  S: A442 OK XPIG-LATIN ompleted-cay
```

[¶](#section-6.5-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#server-responses}
::: {#section-7 .section}
## [7.](#section-7){.section-number .selfRef} [Server Responses](#name-server-responses){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-server-responses}

Server responses are in three forms: status responses, server data, and
command continuation requests. The information contained in a server
response, identified by \"Contents:\" in the response descriptions
below, is described by function, not by syntax. The precise syntax of
server responses is described in \"Formal Syntax\" ([Section
9](#IMAP-ABNF){.xref}).[¶](#section-7-1){.pilcrow}

The client [MUST]{.bcp14} be prepared to accept any response at all
times.[¶](#section-7-2){.pilcrow}

Status responses can be tagged or untagged. Tagged status responses
indicate the completion result (OK, NO, or BAD status) of a client
command and have a tag matching the command.[¶](#section-7-3){.pilcrow}

Some status responses, and all server data, are untagged. An untagged
response is indicated by the token \"\*\" instead of a tag. Untagged
status responses indicate server greeting or server status that does not
indicate the completion of a command (for example, an impending system
shutdown alert). For historical reasons, untagged server data responses
are also called \"unsolicited data\", although strictly speaking, only
unilateral server data is truly
\"unsolicited\".[¶](#section-7-4){.pilcrow}

Certain server data [MUST]{.bcp14} be remembered by the client when it
is received; this is noted in the description of that data. Such data
conveys critical information that affects the interpretation of all
subsequent commands and responses (e.g., updates reflecting the creation
or destruction of messages).[¶](#section-7-5){.pilcrow}

Other server data [SHOULD]{.bcp14} be remembered for later reference; if
the client does not need to remember the data, or if remembering the
data has no obvious purpose (e.g., a SEARCH response when no SEARCH
command is in progress), the data can be
ignored.[¶](#section-7-6){.pilcrow}

An example of unilateral untagged server data occurs when the IMAP
connection is in the selected state. In the selected state, the server
checks the mailbox for new messages as part of command execution.
Normally, this is part of the execution of every command; hence, a NOOP
command suffices to check for new messages. If new messages are found,
the server sends an untagged EXISTS response reflecting the new size of
the mailbox. Server implementations that offer multiple simultaneous
access to the same mailbox [SHOULD]{.bcp14} also send appropriate
unilateral untagged FETCH and EXPUNGE responses if another agent changes
the state of any message flags or expunges any
messages.[¶](#section-7-7){.pilcrow}

Command continuation request responses use the token \"+\" instead of a
tag. These responses are sent by the server to indicate acceptance of an
incomplete client command and readiness for the remainder of the
command.[¶](#section-7-8){.pilcrow}

::: {#server-status-responses}
::: {#section-7.1 .section}
### [7.1.](#section-7.1){.section-number .selfRef} [Server Responses - Generic Status Responses](#name-server-responses-generic-st){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-server-responses-generic-st}

Status responses are OK, NO, BAD, PREAUTH, and BYE. OK, NO, and BAD can
be tagged or untagged. PREAUTH and BYE are always
untagged.[¶](#section-7.1-1){.pilcrow}

Status responses [MAY]{.bcp14} include an [OPTIONAL]{.bcp14} \"response
code\". A response code consists of data inside square brackets in the
form of an atom, possibly followed by a space and arguments. The
response code contains additional information or status codes for client
software beyond the OK/NO/BAD condition and are defined when there is a
specific action that a client can take based upon the additional
information.[¶](#section-7.1-2){.pilcrow}

The currently defined response codes are:[¶](#section-7.1-3){.pilcrow}

[]{.break}

ALERT
:   []{#iref-alert-response-code-130 .iref} The human-readable text
    contains a special alert that is presented to the user in a fashion
    that calls the user\'s attention to the message. Content of ALERT
    response codes received on a connection without TLS or SASL
    security-layer confidentiality [SHOULD]{.bcp14} be ignored by
    clients. If displayed, such alerts [MUST]{.bcp14} be clearly marked
    as potentially suspicious. (Note that some existing clients are
    known to hyperlink returned text, which make them very dangerous.)
    Alerts received after successful establishment of a TLS/SASL
    confidentiality layer [MUST]{.bcp14} be presented to the
    user.[¶](#section-7.1-4.2){.pilcrow}
:   

ALREADYEXISTS

:   []{#iref-alreadyexists-response-code .iref} The operation attempts
    to create something that already exists, such as when a CREATE or
    RENAME command attempts to create a mailbox and there is already one
    of that name.[¶](#section-7.1-4.4.1){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.4.2}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: o356 RENAME this that
      S: o356 NO [ALREADYEXISTS] Mailbox "that" already exists
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.4.2){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

APPENDUID

:   []{#iref-appenduid-response-code-132 .iref} Followed by the
    UIDVALIDITY of the destination mailbox and the UID assigned to the
    appended message in the destination mailbox, it indicates that the
    message has been appended to the destination mailbox with that
    UID.[¶](#section-7.1-4.6.1){.pilcrow}

    If the server also supports the \[[MULTIAPPEND](#RFC3502){.xref}\]
    extension, and if multiple messages were appended in the APPEND
    command, then the second value is a UID set containing the UIDs
    assigned to the appended messages, in the order they were
    transmitted in the APPEND command. This UID set may not contain
    extraneous UIDs or the symbol
    \"\*\".[¶](#section-7.1-4.6.2){.pilcrow}

    Note: the UID set form of the APPENDUID response code [MUST
    NOT]{.bcp14} be used if only a single message was appended. In
    particular, a server [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send a range such as
    123:123. This is because a client that does not support
    \[[MULTIAPPEND](#RFC3502){.xref}\] expects only a single UID and not
    a UID set.[¶](#section-7.1-4.6.3){.pilcrow}

    UIDs are assigned in strictly ascending order in the mailbox (refer
    to [Section 2.3.1.1](#uid-def){.xref}); note that a range of 12:10
    is exactly equivalent to 10:12 and refers to the sequence
    10,11,12.[¶](#section-7.1-4.6.4){.pilcrow}

    This response code is returned in a tagged OK response to the APPEND
    command.[¶](#section-7.1-4.6.5){.pilcrow}

:   

AUTHENTICATIONFAILED

:   []{#iref-authenticationfailed-respon .iref} Authentication failed
    for some reason on which the server is unwilling to elaborate.
    Typically, this includes \"unknown user\" and \"bad
    password\".[¶](#section-7.1-4.8.1){.pilcrow}

    This is the same as not sending any response code, except that when
    a client sees AUTHENTICATIONFAILED, it knows that the problem
    wasn\'t, e.g., UNAVAILABLE, so there\'s no point in trying the same
    login/password again later.[¶](#section-7.1-4.8.2){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.8.3}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: b LOGIN "fred" "foo"
      S: b NO [AUTHENTICATIONFAILED] Authentication failed
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.8.3){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

AUTHORIZATIONFAILED

:   []{#iref-authorizationfailed-respons .iref} Authentication succeeded
    in using the authentication identity, but the server cannot or will
    not allow the authentication identity to act as the requested
    authorization identity. This is only applicable when the
    authentication and authorization identities are
    different.[¶](#section-7.1-4.10.1){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.10.2}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: c1 AUTHENTICATE PLAIN
      [...]
      S: c1 NO [AUTHORIZATIONFAILED] No such authorization-ID
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.10.2){.pilcrow}
    :::

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.10.3}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: c2 AUTHENTICATE PLAIN
      [...]
      S: c2 NO [AUTHORIZATIONFAILED] Authenticator is not an admin
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.10.3){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

BADCHARSET
:   []{#iref-badcharset-response-code-13 .iref} Optionally followed by a
    parenthesized list of charsets. A SEARCH failed because the given
    charset is not supported by this implementation. If the optional
    list of charsets is given, this lists the charsets that are
    supported by this implementation.[¶](#section-7.1-4.12){.pilcrow}
:   

CANNOT

:   []{#iref-cannot-response-code-136 .iref} This operation violates
    some invariant of the server and can never
    succeed.[¶](#section-7.1-4.14.1){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.14.2}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: l create "///////"
      S: l NO [CANNOT] Adjacent slashes are not supported
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.14.2){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

CAPABILITY
:   []{#iref-capability-response-code-13 .iref} Followed by a list of
    capabilities. This can appear in the initial OK or PREAUTH response
    to transmit an initial capabilities list. It can also appear in
    tagged responses to LOGIN or AUTHENTICATE commands. This makes it
    unnecessary for a client to send a separate CAPABILITY command if it
    recognizes this response code and there was no change to the TLS
    and/or authentication state since it was
    received.[¶](#section-7.1-4.16){.pilcrow}
:   

CLIENTBUG

:   []{#iref-clientbug-response-code-138 .iref} The server has detected
    a client bug. This can accompany any of OK, NO, and BAD, depending
    on what the client bug is.[¶](#section-7.1-4.18.1){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.18.2}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: k1 select "/archive/projects/experiment-iv"
      [...]
      S: k1 OK [READ-ONLY] Done
      C: k2 status "/archive/projects/experiment-iv" (messages)
      [...]
      S: k2 OK [CLIENTBUG] Done
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.18.2){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

CLOSED

:   ::: {#closed}
    []{#iref-closed-response-code-139 .iref} The CLOSED response code
    has no parameters. A server returns the CLOSED response code when
    the currently selected mailbox is closed implicitly using the SELECT
    or EXAMINE command on another mailbox. The CLOSED response code
    serves as a boundary between responses for the previously opened
    mailbox (which was closed) and the newly selected mailbox; all
    responses before the CLOSED response code relate to the mailbox that
    was closed, and all subsequent responses relate to the newly opened
    mailbox.[¶](#section-7.1-4.20.1){.pilcrow}

    There is no need to return the CLOSED response code on completion of
    the CLOSE or the UNSELECT command (or similar), whose purpose is to
    close the currently selected mailbox without opening a new
    one.[¶](#section-7.1-4.20.2){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

CONTACTADMIN

:   []{#iref-contactadmin-response-code- .iref} The user should contact
    the system administrator or support
    desk.[¶](#section-7.1-4.22.1){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.22.2}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: e login "fred" "foo"
      S: e NO [CONTACTADMIN]
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.22.2){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

COPYUID

:   []{#iref-copyuid-response-code-141 .iref} Followed by the
    UIDVALIDITY of the destination mailbox, a UID set containing the
    UIDs of the message(s) in the source mailbox that were copied to the
    destination mailbox, followed by another UID set containing the UIDs
    assigned to the copied message(s) in the destination mailbox,
    indicates that the message(s) has been copied to the destination
    mailbox with the stated UID(s).[¶](#section-7.1-4.24.1){.pilcrow}

    The source UID set is in the order the message(s) was copied; the
    destination UID set corresponds to the source UID set and is in the
    same order. Neither of the UID sets may contain extraneous UIDs or
    the symbol \"\*\".[¶](#section-7.1-4.24.2){.pilcrow}

    UIDs are assigned in strictly ascending order in the mailbox (refer
    to [Section 2.3.1.1](#uid-def){.xref}); note that a range of 12:10
    is exactly equivalent to 10:12 and refers to the sequence
    10,11,12.[¶](#section-7.1-4.24.3){.pilcrow}

    This response code is returned in a tagged OK response to the COPY
    or UID COPY command or in the untagged OK response to the MOVE or
    UID MOVE command.[¶](#section-7.1-4.24.4){.pilcrow}

:   

CORRUPTION

:   []{#iref-corruption-response-code-14 .iref} The server discovered
    that some relevant data (e.g., the mailbox) are corrupt. This
    response code does not include any information about what\'s
    corrupt, but the server can write that to its
    logfiles.[¶](#section-7.1-4.26.1){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.26.2}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: i select "/archive/projects/experiment-iv"
      S: i NO [CORRUPTION] Cannot open mailbox
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.26.2){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

EXPIRED

:   []{#iref-expired-response-code-143 .iref} Either authentication
    succeeded or the server no longer had the necessary data; either
    way, access is no longer permitted using that passphrase. The client
    or user should get a new
    passphrase.[¶](#section-7.1-4.28.1){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.28.2}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: d login "fred" "foo"
      S: d NO [EXPIRED] That password isn't valid any more
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.28.2){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

EXPUNGEISSUED

:   []{#iref-expungeissued-response-code .iref} Someone else has issued
    an EXPUNGE for the same mailbox. The client may want to issue NOOP
    soon. \[[IMAP-MULTIACCESS](#RFC2180){.xref}\] discusses this subject
    in depth.[¶](#section-7.1-4.30.1){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.30.2}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: h search from maria@example.com
      S: * ESEARCH (TAG "h") ALL 1:3,5,8,13,21,42
      S: h OK [EXPUNGEISSUED] Search completed
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.30.2){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

HASCHILDREN

:   []{#iref-haschildren-response-code-1 .iref} The mailbox delete
    operation failed because the mailbox has one or more children, and
    the server doesn\'t allow deletion of mailboxes with
    children.[¶](#section-7.1-4.32.1){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.32.2}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: m356 DELETE Notes
      S: o356 NO [HASCHILDREN] Mailbox "Notes" has children
      that need to be deleted first
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.32.2){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

INUSE

:   []{#iref-inuse-response-code-146 .iref} An operation has not been
    carried out because it involves sawing off a branch someone else is
    sitting on. Someone else may be holding an exclusive lock needed for
    this operation, or the operation may involve deleting a resource
    someone else is using, typically a
    mailbox.[¶](#section-7.1-4.34.1){.pilcrow}

    The operation may succeed if the client tries again
    later.[¶](#section-7.1-4.34.2){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.34.3}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: g delete "/archive/projects/experiment-iv"
      S: g NO [INUSE] Mailbox in use
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.34.3){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

LIMIT

:   []{#iref-limit-response-code-147 .iref} The operation ran up against
    an implementation limit of some kind, such as the number of flags on
    a single message or the number of flags used in a
    mailbox.[¶](#section-7.1-4.36.1){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.36.2}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: m STORE 42 FLAGS f1 f2 f3 f4 f5 ... f250
      S: m NO [LIMIT] At most 32 flags in one mailbox supported
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.36.2){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

NONEXISTENT

:   []{#iref-nonexistent-response-code-1 .iref} The operation attempts
    to delete something that does not exist. Similar to
    ALREADYEXISTS.[¶](#section-7.1-4.38.1){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.38.2}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: p RENAME this that
      S: p NO [NONEXISTENT] No such mailbox
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.38.2){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

NOPERM

:   []{#iref-noperm-response-code-149 .iref} The access control system
    (e.g., ACL; see \[[RFC4314](#RFC4314){.xref}\]) does not permit this
    user to carry out an operation, such as selecting or creating a
    mailbox.[¶](#section-7.1-4.40.1){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.40.2}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: f select "/archive/projects/experiment-iv"
      S: f NO [NOPERM] Access denied
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.40.2){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

OVERQUOTA

:   []{#iref-overquota-response-code-150 .iref} The user would be over
    quota after the operation. (The user may or may not be over quota
    already.)[¶](#section-7.1-4.42.1){.pilcrow}

    Note that if the server sends OVERQUOTA but doesn\'t support the
    IMAP QUOTA extension defined by \[[RFC2087](#RFC2087){.xref}\], then
    there is a quota, but the client cannot find out what the quota
    is.[¶](#section-7.1-4.42.2){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.42.3}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: n1 uid copy 1:* oldmail
      S: n1 NO [OVERQUOTA] Sorry
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.42.3){.pilcrow}
    :::

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.42.4}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: n2 uid copy 1:* oldmail
      S: n2 OK [OVERQUOTA] You are now over your soft quota
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.42.4){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

PARSE
:   []{#iref-parse-response-code-151 .iref} The human-readable text
    represents an error in parsing the \[[RFC5322](#RFC5322){.xref}\]
    header or \[[MIME-IMB](#RFC2045){.xref}\] headers of a message in
    the mailbox.[¶](#section-7.1-4.44){.pilcrow}
:   

PERMANENTFLAGS

:   []{#iref-permanentflags-response-cod .iref} Followed by a
    parenthesized list of flags and indicates which of the known flags
    the client can change permanently. Any flags that are in the FLAGS
    untagged response, but not in the PERMANENTFLAGS list, cannot be set
    permanently. The PERMANENTFLAGS list can also include the special
    flag \\\*, which indicates that it is possible to create new
    keywords by attempting to store those keywords in the mailbox. If
    the client attempts to STORE a flag that is not in the
    PERMANENTFLAGS list, the server will either ignore the change or
    store the state change for the remainder of the current session
    only.[¶](#section-7.1-4.46.1){.pilcrow}

    There is no need for a server that included the special flag \\\* to
    return a new PERMANENTFLAGS response code when a new keyword was
    successfully set on a message upon client request. However, if the
    server has a limit on the number of different keywords that can be
    stored in a mailbox and that limit is reached, the server
    [MUST]{.bcp14} send a new PERMANENTFLAGS response code without the
    special flag \\\*.[¶](#section-7.1-4.46.2){.pilcrow}

:   

PRIVACYREQUIRED

:   []{#iref-privacyrequired-response-co .iref} The operation is not
    permitted due to a lack of data confidentiality. If TLS is not in
    use, the client could try STARTTLS (see [Section
    6.2.1](#STARTTLS){.xref}) or alternatively reconnect on an Implicit
    TLS port, and then repeat the
    operation.[¶](#section-7.1-4.48.1){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.48.2}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: d login "fred" "foo"
      S: d NO [PRIVACYREQUIRED] Connection offers no privacy
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.48.2){.pilcrow}
    :::

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.48.3}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: d select inbox
      S: d NO [PRIVACYREQUIRED] Connection offers no privacy
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.48.3){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

READ-ONLY
:   []{#iref-read-only-response-code-154 .iref} The mailbox is selected
    as read-only, or its access while selected has changed from
    read-write to read-only.[¶](#section-7.1-4.50){.pilcrow}
:   

READ-WRITE
:   []{#iref-read-write-response-code-15 .iref} The mailbox is selected
    as read-write, or its access while selected has changed from
    read-only to read-write.[¶](#section-7.1-4.52){.pilcrow}
:   

SERVERBUG

:   []{#iref-serverbug-response-code-156 .iref} The server encountered a
    bug in itself or violated one of its own
    invariants.[¶](#section-7.1-4.54.1){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.54.2}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: j select "/archive/projects/experiment-iv"
      S: j NO [SERVERBUG] This should not happen
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.54.2){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

TRYCREATE
:   []{#iref-trycreate-response-code-157 .iref} An APPEND, COPY, or MOVE
    attempt is failing because the target mailbox does not exist (as
    opposed to some other reason). This is a hint to the client that the
    operation can succeed if the mailbox is first created by the CREATE
    command.[¶](#section-7.1-4.56){.pilcrow}
:   

UIDNEXT
:   []{#iref-uidnext-response-code-158 .iref} Followed by a decimal
    number and indicates the next unique identifier value. Refer to
    [Section 2.3.1.1](#uid-def){.xref} for more
    information.[¶](#section-7.1-4.58){.pilcrow}
:   

UIDNOTSTICKY

:   []{#iref-uidnotsticky-response-code- .iref} The selected mailbox is
    supported by a mail store that does not support persistent UIDs;
    that is, UIDVALIDITY will be different each time the mailbox is
    selected. Consequently, APPEND or COPY to this mailbox will not
    return an APPENDUID or COPYUID response
    code.[¶](#section-7.1-4.60.1){.pilcrow}

    This response code is returned in an untagged NO response to the
    SELECT command.[¶](#section-7.1-4.60.2){.pilcrow}

    Note: servers [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} have any UIDNOTSTICKY mail
    stores. This facility exists to support legacy mail stores in which
    it is technically infeasible to support persistent UIDs. This should
    be avoided when designing new mail
    stores.[¶](#section-7.1-4.60.3){.pilcrow}

:   

UIDVALIDITY
:   []{#iref-uidvalidity-response-code-1 .iref} Followed by a decimal
    number and indicates the unique identifier validity value. Refer to
    [Section 2.3.1.1](#uid-def){.xref} for more
    information.[¶](#section-7.1-4.62){.pilcrow}
:   

UNAVAILABLE

:   []{#iref-unavailable-response-code-1 .iref} Temporary failure
    because a subsystem is down. For example, an IMAP server that uses a
    Lightweight Directory Access Protocol (LDAP) or Radius server for
    authentication might use this response code when the LDAP/Radius
    server is down.[¶](#section-7.1-4.64.1){.pilcrow}

    ::: {#section-7.1-4.64.2}
    ``` sourcecode
      C: a LOGIN "fred" "foo"
      S: a NO [UNAVAILABLE] User's backend down for maintenance
    ```

    [¶](#section-7.1-4.64.2){.pilcrow}
    :::

:   

UNKNOWN-CTE
:   []{#iref-unknown-cte-response-code-1 .iref} The server does not know
    how to decode the section\'s
    Content-Transfer-Encoding.[¶](#section-7.1-4.66){.pilcrow}
:   

Client implementations [MUST]{.bcp14} ignore response codes that they do
not recognize.[¶](#section-7.1-5){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.1.1 .section}
#### [7.1.1.](#section-7.1.1){.section-number .selfRef} [OK Response](#name-ok-response){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-ok-response}

[]{#iref-ok-response-163 .iref} []{.break}

Contents:

:   -   [[OPTIONAL]{.bcp14} response
        code[¶](#section-7.1.1-1.2.1.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.1.1-1.2.1.1}
    -   [human-readable
        text[¶](#section-7.1.1-1.2.1.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.1.1-1.2.1.2}

:   

The OK response indicates an information message from the server. When
tagged, it indicates successful completion of the associated command.
The human-readable text [MAY]{.bcp14} be presented to the user as an
information message. The untagged form indicates an information-only
message; the nature of the information [MAY]{.bcp14} be indicated by a
response code.[¶](#section-7.1.1-2){.pilcrow}

The untagged form is also used as one of three possible greetings at
connection startup. It indicates that the connection is not yet
authenticated and that a LOGIN or an AUTHENTICATE command is
needed.[¶](#section-7.1.1-3){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-7.1.1-4){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.1.1-5}
``` sourcecode
  S: * OK IMAP4rev2 server ready
  C: A001 LOGIN fred blurdybloop
  S: * OK [ALERT] System shutdown in 10 minutes
  S: A001 OK LOGIN Completed
```

[¶](#section-7.1.1-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#section-7.1.2 .section}
#### [7.1.2.](#section-7.1.2){.section-number .selfRef} [NO Response](#name-no-response){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-no-response}

[]{#iref-no-response-164 .iref} []{.break}

Contents:

:   -   [[OPTIONAL]{.bcp14} response
        code[¶](#section-7.1.2-1.2.1.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.1.2-1.2.1.1}
    -   [human-readable
        text[¶](#section-7.1.2-1.2.1.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.1.2-1.2.1.2}

:   

The NO response indicates an operational error message from the server.
When tagged, it indicates unsuccessful completion of the associated
command. The untagged form indicates a warning; the command can still
complete successfully. The human-readable text describes the
condition.[¶](#section-7.1.2-2){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-7.1.2-3){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.1.2-4}
``` sourcecode
  C: A222 COPY 1:2 owatagusiam
  S: * NO Disk is 98% full, please delete unnecessary data
  S: A222 OK COPY completed
  C: A223 COPY 3:200 blurdybloop
  S: * NO Disk is 98% full, please delete unnecessary data
  S: * NO Disk is 99% full, please delete unnecessary data
  S: A223 NO COPY failed: disk is full
```

[¶](#section-7.1.2-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#section-7.1.3 .section}
#### [7.1.3.](#section-7.1.3){.section-number .selfRef} [BAD Response](#name-bad-response){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-bad-response}

[]{#iref-bad-response-165 .iref} []{.break}

Contents:

:   -   [[OPTIONAL]{.bcp14} response
        code[¶](#section-7.1.3-1.2.1.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.1.3-1.2.1.1}
    -   [human-readable
        text[¶](#section-7.1.3-1.2.1.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.1.3-1.2.1.2}

:   

The BAD response indicates an error message from the server. When
tagged, it reports a protocol-level error in the client\'s command; the
tag indicates the command that caused the error. The untagged form
indicates a protocol-level error for which the associated command can
not be determined; it can also indicate an internal server failure. The
human-readable text describes the
condition.[¶](#section-7.1.3-2){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-7.1.3-3){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.1.3-4}
``` sourcecode
  C: ...very long command line...
  S: * BAD Command line too long
  C: ...empty line...
  S: * BAD Empty command line
  C: A443 EXPUNGE
  S: * BAD Disk crash, attempting salvage to a new disk!
  S: * OK Salvage successful, no data lost
  S: A443 OK Expunge completed
```

[¶](#section-7.1.3-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#preauth-resp}
::: {#section-7.1.4 .section}
#### [7.1.4.](#section-7.1.4){.section-number .selfRef} [PREAUTH Response](#name-preauth-response){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-preauth-response}

[]{#iref-preauth-response-166 .iref} []{.break}

Contents:

:   -   [[OPTIONAL]{.bcp14} response
        code[¶](#section-7.1.4-1.2.1.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.1.4-1.2.1.1}
    -   [human-readable
        text[¶](#section-7.1.4-1.2.1.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.1.4-1.2.1.2}

:   

The PREAUTH response is always untagged and is one of three possible
greetings at connection startup. It indicates that the connection has
already been authenticated by external means; thus, no
LOGIN/AUTHENTICATE command is needed.[¶](#section-7.1.4-2){.pilcrow}

Because PREAUTH moves the connection directly to the authenticated
state, it effectively prevents the client from using the STARTTLS
command ([Section 6.2.1](#STARTTLS){.xref}). For this reason, the
PREAUTH response [SHOULD]{.bcp14} only be returned by servers on
connections that are protected by TLS (such as on an Implicit TLS port
\[[RFC8314](#RFC8314){.xref}\]) or protected through other means such as
IPsec. Clients that require mandatory TLS [MUST]{.bcp14} close the
connection after receiving the PREAUTH response on a non-protected
port.[¶](#section-7.1.4-3){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-7.1.4-4){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.1.4-5}
``` sourcecode
  S: * PREAUTH IMAP4rev2 server logged in as Smith
```

[¶](#section-7.1.4-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::

::: {#section-7.1.5 .section}
#### [7.1.5.](#section-7.1.5){.section-number .selfRef} [BYE Response](#name-bye-response){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-bye-response}

[]{#iref-bye-response-167 .iref} []{.break}

Contents:

:   -   [[OPTIONAL]{.bcp14} response
        code[¶](#section-7.1.5-1.2.1.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.1.5-1.2.1.1}
    -   [human-readable
        text[¶](#section-7.1.5-1.2.1.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.1.5-1.2.1.2}

:   

The BYE response is always untagged and indicates that the server is
about to close the connection. The human-readable text [MAY]{.bcp14} be
displayed to the user in a status report by the client. The BYE response
is sent under one of four conditions:[¶](#section-7.1.5-2){.pilcrow}

1.  [as part of a normal logout sequence. The server will close the
    connection after sending the tagged OK response to the LOGOUT
    command.[¶](#section-7.1.5-3.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.1.5-3.1}
2.  [as a panic shutdown announcement. The server closes the connection
    immediately.[¶](#section-7.1.5-3.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.1.5-3.2}
3.  [as an announcement of an inactivity autologout. The server closes
    the connection
    immediately.[¶](#section-7.1.5-3.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.1.5-3.3}
4.  [as one of three possible greetings at connection startup,
    indicating that the server is not willing to accept a connection
    from this client. The server closes the connection
    immediately.[¶](#section-7.1.5-3.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.1.5-3.4}

The difference between a BYE that occurs as part of a normal LOGOUT
sequence (the first case) and a BYE that occurs because of a failure
(the other three cases) is that the connection closes immediately in the
failure case. In all cases, the client [SHOULD]{.bcp14} continue to read
response data from the server until the connection is closed; this will
ensure that any pending untagged or completion responses are read and
processed.[¶](#section-7.1.5-4){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-7.1.5-5){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.1.5-6}
``` sourcecode
  S: * BYE Autologout; idle for too long
```

[¶](#section-7.1.5-6){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#server-status-capabilities}
::: {#section-7.2 .section}
### [7.2.](#section-7.2){.section-number .selfRef} [Server Responses - Server Status](#name-server-responses-server-sta){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-server-responses-server-sta}

These responses are always untagged. This is how server status data are
transmitted from the server to the client.[¶](#section-7.2-1){.pilcrow}

::: {#enabled}
::: {#section-7.2.1 .section}
#### [7.2.1.](#section-7.2.1){.section-number .selfRef} [ENABLED Response](#name-enabled-response){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-enabled-response}

[]{.break}

Contents:
:   capability listing[¶](#section-7.2.1-1.2){.pilcrow}
:   

The ENABLED response occurs as a result of an ENABLE command. The
capability listing contains a space-separated listing of capability
names that the server supports and that were successfully enabled. The
ENABLED response may contain no capabilities, which means that no
extensions listed by the client were successfully
enabled.[¶](#section-7.2.1-2){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-7.2.1-3){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.2.1-4}
``` sourcecode
  S: * ENABLED CONDSTORE QRESYNC
```

[¶](#section-7.2.1-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::

::: {#capability-resp}
::: {#section-7.2.2 .section}
#### [7.2.2.](#section-7.2.2){.section-number .selfRef} [CAPABILITY Response](#name-capability-response){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-capability-response}

[]{#iref-capability-response-168 .iref} []{.break}

Contents:
:   capability listing[¶](#section-7.2.2-1.2){.pilcrow}
:   

The CAPABILITY response occurs as a result of a CAPABILITY command. The
capability listing contains a space-separated listing of capability
names that the server supports. The capability listing [MUST]{.bcp14}
include the atom \"IMAP4rev2\", but note that it doesn\'t have to be the
first capability listed. The order of capability names has no
significance.[¶](#section-7.2.2-2){.pilcrow}

Client and server implementations [MUST]{.bcp14} implement the
capabilities \"AUTH=PLAIN\" (described in \[[PLAIN](#RFC4616){.xref}\]),
and [MUST]{.bcp14} implement \"STARTTLS\" and \"LOGINDISABLED\" on the
cleartext port. See the Security Considerations ([Section
11](#sec-cons){.xref}) for important information related to these
capabilities.[¶](#section-7.2.2-3){.pilcrow}

A capability name that begins with \"AUTH=\" indicates that the server
supports that particular authentication mechanism
\[[SASL](#RFC4422){.xref}\].[¶](#section-7.2.2-4){.pilcrow}

The LOGINDISABLED capability indicates that the LOGIN command is
disabled, and that the server will respond with a tagged NO response to
any attempt to use the LOGIN command even if the user name and password
are valid (their validity will not be checked). An IMAP client [MUST
NOT]{.bcp14} issue the LOGIN command if the server advertises the
LOGINDISABLED capability.[¶](#section-7.2.2-5){.pilcrow}

Other capability names indicate that the server supports an extension,
revision, or amendment to the IMAP4rev2 protocol. If IMAP4rev1
capability is not advertised, server responses [MUST]{.bcp14} conform to
this document until the client issues a command that uses an additional
capability. If both IMAP4rev1 and IMAP4rev2 capabilities are advertised,
server responses [MUST]{.bcp14} conform to
\[[RFC3501](#RFC3501){.xref}\] until the client issues a command that
uses an additional capability. (For example, the client can issue ENABLE
IMAP4rev2 to enable IMAP4rev2-specific
behavior.)[¶](#section-7.2.2-6){.pilcrow}

Capability names [SHOULD]{.bcp14} be registered with IANA using the RFC
Required policy \[[RFC8126](#RFC8126){.xref}\]. A server [SHOULD
NOT]{.bcp14} offer unregistered capability
names.[¶](#section-7.2.2-7){.pilcrow}

Client implementations [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} require any capability name
other than \"IMAP4rev2\", and possibly \"STARTTLS\" and
\"LOGINDISABLED\" (on a cleartext port). Client implementations
[MUST]{.bcp14} ignore any unknown capability
names.[¶](#section-7.2.2-8){.pilcrow}

A server [MAY]{.bcp14} send capabilities automatically, by using the
CAPABILITY response code in the initial PREAUTH or OK responses and by
sending an updated CAPABILITY response code in the tagged OK response as
part of a successful authentication. It is unnecessary for a client to
send a separate CAPABILITY command if it recognizes these automatic
capabilities and there was no change to the TLS and/or authentication
state since they were received.[¶](#section-7.2.2-9){.pilcrow}

The list of capabilities returned by a server [MAY]{.bcp14} change
during the connection. In particular, it is quite common for the server
to change the list of capabilities after successful TLS negotiation
(STARTTLS command) and/or after successful authentication (AUTHENTICATE
or LOGIN commands).[¶](#section-7.2.2-10){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-7.2.2-11){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.2.2-12}
``` sourcecode
  S: * CAPABILITY STARTTLS AUTH=GSSAPI IMAP4rev2 LOGINDISABLED
   XPIG-LATIN
```

[¶](#section-7.2.2-12){.pilcrow}
:::

Note that in the above example, XPIG-LATIN is a fictitious capability
name.[¶](#section-7.2.2-13){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#section-7.3 .section}
### [7.3.](#section-7.3){.section-number .selfRef} [Server Responses - Mailbox Status](#name-server-responses-mailbox-st){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-server-responses-mailbox-st}

These responses are always untagged. This is how mailbox status data are
transmitted from the server to the client. Many of these responses
typically result from a command with the same
name.[¶](#section-7.3-1){.pilcrow}

::: {#list-resp}
::: {#section-7.3.1 .section}
#### [7.3.1.](#section-7.3.1){.section-number .selfRef} [LIST Response](#name-list-response){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-list-response}

[]{#iref-list-response-169 .iref} []{.break}

Contents:

:   -   [name
        attributes[¶](#section-7.3.1-1.2.1.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.3.1-1.2.1.1}
    -   [hierarchy
        delimiter[¶](#section-7.3.1-1.2.1.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.3.1-1.2.1.2}
    -   [name[¶](#section-7.3.1-1.2.1.3){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.3.1-1.2.1.3}
    -   [[OPTIONAL]{.bcp14} extension
        data[¶](#section-7.3.1-1.2.1.4){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.3.1-1.2.1.4}

:   

The LIST response occurs as a result of a LIST command. It returns a
single name that matches the LIST specification. There can be multiple
LIST responses for a single LIST command.[¶](#section-7.3.1-2){.pilcrow}

The following base mailbox name attributes are
defined:[¶](#section-7.3.1-3){.pilcrow}

[]{.break}

\\NonExistent

:   []{#iref-nonexistent-mailbox-name-at .iref} The \"\\NonExistent\"
    attribute indicates that a mailbox name does not refer to an
    existing mailbox. Note that this attribute is not meaningful by
    itself, as mailbox names that match the canonical LIST pattern but
    don\'t exist must not be returned unless one of the two conditions
    listed below is also satisfied:[¶](#section-7.3.1-4.2.1){.pilcrow}

    1.  [The mailbox name also satisfies the selection criteria (for
        example, it is subscribed and the \"SUBSCRIBED\" selection
        option has been
        specified).[¶](#section-7.3.1-4.2.2.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.3.1-4.2.2.1}
    2.  [\"RECURSIVEMATCH\" has been specified, and the mailbox name has
        at least one descendant mailbox name that does not match the
        LIST pattern and does match the selection
        criteria.[¶](#section-7.3.1-4.2.2.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.3.1-4.2.2.2}

    In practice, this means that the \"\\NonExistent\" attribute is
    usually returned with one or more of \"\\Subscribed\", \"\\Remote\",
    \"\\HasChildren\", or the CHILDINFO extended data
    item.[¶](#section-7.3.1-4.2.3){.pilcrow}

    The \"\\NonExistent\" attribute implies
    \"\\NoSelect\".[¶](#section-7.3.1-4.2.4){.pilcrow}

:   

\\Noinferiors
:   []{#iref-noinferiors-mailbox-name-at .iref} It is not possible for
    any child levels of hierarchy to exist under this name; no child
    levels exist now and none can be created in the
    future.[¶](#section-7.3.1-4.4){.pilcrow}
:   

\\Noselect
:   []{#iref-noselect-mailbox-name-attri .iref} It is not possible to
    use this name as a selectable
    mailbox.[¶](#section-7.3.1-4.6){.pilcrow}
:   

\\HasChildren
:   []{#iref-haschildren-mailbox-name-at .iref} The presence of this
    attribute indicates that the mailbox has child mailboxes. A server
    [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} set this attribute if there are child mailboxes
    and the user does not have permission to access any of them. In this
    case, \\HasNoChildren [SHOULD]{.bcp14} be used. In many cases,
    however, a server may not be able to efficiently compute whether a
    user has access to any child mailboxes. Note that even though the
    \\HasChildren attribute for a mailbox must be correct at the time of
    processing the mailbox, a client must be prepared to deal with a
    situation when a mailbox is marked with the \\HasChildren attribute,
    but no child mailbox appears in the response to the LIST command.
    This might happen, for example, due to child mailboxes being deleted
    or made inaccessible to the user (using access control) by another
    client before the server is able to list
    them.[¶](#section-7.3.1-4.8){.pilcrow}
:   

\\HasNoChildren
:   []{#iref-hasnochildren-mailbox-name- .iref} The presence of this
    attribute indicates that the mailbox has NO child mailboxes that are
    accessible to the currently authenticated
    user.[¶](#section-7.3.1-4.10){.pilcrow}
:   

\\Marked
:   []{#iref-marked-mailbox-name-attribu .iref} The mailbox has been
    marked \"interesting\" by the server; the mailbox probably contains
    messages that have been added since the last time the mailbox was
    selected.[¶](#section-7.3.1-4.12){.pilcrow}
:   

\\Unmarked
:   []{#iref-unmarked-mailbox-name-attri .iref} The mailbox does not
    contain any additional messages since the last time the mailbox was
    selected.[¶](#section-7.3.1-4.14){.pilcrow}
:   

\\Subscribed
:   []{#iref-subscribed-mailbox-name-att .iref} The mailbox name was
    subscribed to using the SUBSCRIBE
    command.[¶](#section-7.3.1-4.16){.pilcrow}
:   

\\Remote
:   []{#iref-remote-mailbox-name-attribu .iref} The mailbox is a remote
    mailbox.[¶](#section-7.3.1-4.18){.pilcrow}
:   

It is an error for the server to return both a \\HasChildren and a
\\HasNoChildren attribute in the same LIST response. A client that
encounters a LIST response with both \\HasChildren and \\HasNoChildren
attributes present should act as if both are absent in the LIST
response.[¶](#section-7.3.1-5){.pilcrow}

Note: the \\HasNoChildren attribute should not be confused with the
\\NoInferiors attribute, which indicates that no child mailboxes exist
now and none can be created in the
future.[¶](#section-7.3.1-6){.pilcrow}

If it is not feasible for the server to determine whether or not the
mailbox is \"interesting\", the server [SHOULD NOT]{.bcp14} send either
\\Marked or \\Unmarked. The server [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} send more than one
of \\Marked, \\Unmarked, and \\Noselect for a single mailbox, and it
[MAY]{.bcp14} send none of these.[¶](#section-7.3.1-7){.pilcrow}

In addition to the base mailbox name attributes defined above, an IMAP
server [MAY]{.bcp14} also include any or all of the following attributes
that denote \"role\" (or \"special-use\") of a mailbox. These attributes
are included along with base attributes defined above. A given mailbox
may have none, one, or more than one of these attributes. In some cases,
a special use is advice to a client about what to put in that mailbox.
In other cases, it\'s advice to a client about what to expect to find
there.[¶](#section-7.3.1-8){.pilcrow}

[]{.break}

\\All
:   []{#iref-all-mailbox-name-attribute- .iref} This mailbox presents
    all messages in the user\'s message store. Implementations
    [MAY]{.bcp14} omit some messages, such as, perhaps, those in \\Trash
    and \\Junk. When this special use is supported, it is almost certain
    to represent a virtual mailbox.[¶](#section-7.3.1-9.2){.pilcrow}
:   

\\Archive
:   []{#iref-archive-mailbox-name-attrib .iref} This mailbox is used to
    archive messages. The meaning of an \"archival\" mailbox is server
    dependent; typically, it will be used to get messages out of the
    inbox, or otherwise keep them out of the user\'s way, while still
    making them accessible.[¶](#section-7.3.1-9.4){.pilcrow}
:   

\\Drafts
:   []{#iref-drafts-mailbox-name-attribu .iref} This mailbox is used to
    hold draft messages \-- typically, messages that are being composed
    but have not yet been sent. In some server implementations, this
    might be a virtual mailbox, containing messages from other mailboxes
    that are marked with the \"\\Draft\" message flag. Alternatively,
    this might just be advice that a client put drafts
    here.[¶](#section-7.3.1-9.6){.pilcrow}
:   

\\Flagged
:   []{#iref-flagged-mailbox-name-attrib .iref} This mailbox presents
    all messages marked in some way as \"important\". When this special
    use is supported, it is likely to represent a virtual mailbox
    collecting messages (from other mailboxes) that are marked with the
    \"\\Flagged\" message flag.[¶](#section-7.3.1-9.8){.pilcrow}
:   

\\Junk
:   []{#iref-junk-mailbox-name-attribute .iref} This mailbox is where
    messages deemed to be junk mail are held. Some server
    implementations might put messages here automatically.
    Alternatively, this might just be advice to a client-side spam
    filter.[¶](#section-7.3.1-9.10){.pilcrow}
:   

\\Sent
:   []{#iref-sent-mailbox-name-attribute .iref} This mailbox is used to
    hold copies of messages that have been sent. Some server
    implementations might put messages here automatically.
    Alternatively, this might just be advice that a client save sent
    messages here.[¶](#section-7.3.1-9.12){.pilcrow}
:   

\\Trash
:   []{#iref-trash-mailbox-name-attribut .iref} This mailbox is used to
    hold messages that have been deleted or marked for deletion. In some
    server implementations, this might be a virtual mailbox, containing
    messages from other mailboxes that are marked with the \"\\Deleted\"
    message flag. Alternatively, this might just be advice that a client
    that chooses not to use the IMAP \"\\Deleted\" model should use as
    its trash location. In server implementations that strictly expect
    the IMAP \"\\Deleted\" model, this special use is likely not to be
    supported.[¶](#section-7.3.1-9.14){.pilcrow}
:   

All special-use attributes are [OPTIONAL]{.bcp14}, and any given server
or message store may support any combination of the attributes, or none
at all. In most cases, there will likely be at most one mailbox with a
given attribute for a given user, but in some server or message store
implementations, it might be possible for multiple mailboxes to have the
same special-use attribute.[¶](#section-7.3.1-10){.pilcrow}

Special-use attributes are likely to be user specific. User Adam might
share his \\Sent mailbox with user Barb, but that mailbox is unlikely to
also serve as Barb\'s \\Sent mailbox.[¶](#section-7.3.1-11){.pilcrow}

Other mailbox name attributes can be found in the \"IMAP Mailbox Name
Attributes\" registry
\[[IMAP-MAILBOX-NAME-ATTRS-REG](#IMAP-MAILBOX-NAME-ATTRS-REG){.xref}\].[¶](#section-7.3.1-12){.pilcrow}

The hierarchy delimiter is a character used to delimit levels of
hierarchy in a mailbox name. A client can use it to create child
mailboxes and to search higher or lower levels of naming hierarchy. All
children of a top-level hierarchy node [MUST]{.bcp14} use the same
separator character. A NIL hierarchy delimiter means that no hierarchy
exists; the name is a \"flat\" name.[¶](#section-7.3.1-13){.pilcrow}

The name represents an unambiguous left-to-right hierarchy and
[MUST]{.bcp14} be valid for use as a reference in LIST command. Unless
\\Noselect or \\NonExistent is indicated, the name [MUST]{.bcp14} also
be valid as an argument for commands, such as SELECT, that accept
mailbox names.[¶](#section-7.3.1-14){.pilcrow}

The name might be followed by an [OPTIONAL]{.bcp14} series of extended
fields, a parenthesized list of tagged data (also referred to as an
\"extended data item\"). The first element of an extended field is a
string, which identifies the type of data.
\[[RFC5258](#RFC5258){.xref}\] specifies requirements on string
registration (which are called \"tags\"; such tags are not to be
confused with IMAP command tags); in particular, it states that \"Tags
[MUST]{.bcp14} be registered with IANA\". This document doesn\'t change
that. See [Section
9.5](https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc5258#section-9.5){.relref} of
\[[RFC5258](#RFC5258){.xref}\] for the registration template. The server
[MAY]{.bcp14} return data in the extended fields that was not directly
solicited by the client in the corresponding LIST command. For example,
the client can enable extra extended fields by using another IMAP
extension that makes use of the extended LIST responses. The client
[MUST]{.bcp14} ignore all extended fields it doesn\'t
recognize.[¶](#section-7.3.1-15){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-7.3.1-16){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.3.1-17}
``` sourcecode
  S: * LIST (\Noselect) "/" ~/Mail/foo
```

[¶](#section-7.3.1-17){.pilcrow}
:::

Example:[¶](#section-7.3.1-18){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.3.1-19}
``` sourcecode
  S: * LIST (\Marked) ":" Tables (tablecloth (("edge" "lacy")
      ("color" "red")) Sample "text")
  S: * LIST () ":" Tables:new (tablecloth ("edge" "lacy")
      Sample ("text" "more text"))
```

[¶](#section-7.3.1-19){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::

::: {#section-7.3.2 .section}
#### [7.3.2.](#section-7.3.2){.section-number .selfRef} [NAMESPACE Response](#name-namespace-response){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-namespace-response}

[]{#iref-namespace-response-186 .iref} []{.break}

Contents:
:   the prefix and hierarchy delimiter to the server\'s Personal
    Namespace(s), Other Users\' Namespace(s), and Shared
    Namespace(s)[¶](#section-7.3.2-1.2){.pilcrow}
:   

The NAMESPACE response occurs as a result of a NAMESPACE command. It
contains the prefix and hierarchy delimiter to the server\'s Personal
Namespace(s), Other Users\' Namespace(s), and Shared Namespace(s) that
the server wishes to expose. The response will contain a NIL for any
namespace class that is not available. The Namespace-Response-Extensions
ABNF non-terminal is defined for extensibility and [MAY]{.bcp14} be
included in the response.[¶](#section-7.3.2-2){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-7.3.2-3){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.3.2-4}
``` sourcecode
  S: * NAMESPACE (("" "/")) (("~" "/")) NIL
```

[¶](#section-7.3.2-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#section-7.3.3 .section}
#### [7.3.3.](#section-7.3.3){.section-number .selfRef} [STATUS Response](#name-status-response){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-status-response}

[]{#iref-status-response-187 .iref} []{.break}

Contents:

:   -   [name[¶](#section-7.3.3-1.2.1.1){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.3.3-1.2.1.1}
    -   [status parenthesized
        list[¶](#section-7.3.3-1.2.1.2){.pilcrow}]{#section-7.3.3-1.2.1.2}

:   

The STATUS response occurs as a result of a STATUS command. It returns
the mailbox name that matches the STATUS specification and the requested
mailbox status information.[¶](#section-7.3.3-2){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-7.3.3-3){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.3.3-4}
``` sourcecode
  S: * STATUS blurdybloop (MESSAGES 231 UIDNEXT 44292)
```

[¶](#section-7.3.3-4){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#section-7.3.4 .section}
#### [7.3.4.](#section-7.3.4){.section-number .selfRef} [ESEARCH Response](#name-esearch-response){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-esearch-response}

[]{#iref-esearch-response-188 .iref} []{.break}

Contents:
:   one or more search-return-data
    pairs[¶](#section-7.3.4-1.2){.pilcrow}
:   

The ESEARCH response occurs as a result of a SEARCH or UID SEARCH
command.[¶](#section-7.3.4-2){.pilcrow}

The ESEARCH response starts with an optional search correlator. If it is
missing, then the response was not caused by a particular IMAP command,
whereas if it is present, it contains the tag of the command that caused
the response to be returned.[¶](#section-7.3.4-3){.pilcrow}

The search correlator is followed by an optional UID indicator. If this
indicator is present, all data in the ESEARCH response refers to UIDs;
otherwise, all returned data refers to message
numbers.[¶](#section-7.3.4-4){.pilcrow}

The rest of the ESEARCH response contains one or more search data pairs.
Each pair starts with a unique return item name, followed by a space and
the corresponding data. Search data pairs may be returned in any order.
Unless otherwise specified by an extension, any return item name
[SHOULD]{.bcp14} appear only once in an ESEARCH
response.[¶](#section-7.3.4-5){.pilcrow}

This document specifies the following return item
names:[¶](#section-7.3.4-6){.pilcrow}

[]{.break}

MIN

:   []{#iref-min-search-return-item-name .iref} Returns the lowest
    message number/UID that satisfies the SEARCH
    criteria.[¶](#section-7.3.4-7.2.1){.pilcrow}

    If the SEARCH results in no matches, the server [MUST NOT]{.bcp14}
    include the MIN return item in the ESEARCH response; however, it
    still [MUST]{.bcp14} send the ESEARCH
    response.[¶](#section-7.3.4-7.2.2){.pilcrow}

:   

MAX

:   []{#iref-max-search-return-item-name .iref} Returns the highest
    message number/UID that satisfies the SEARCH
    criteria.[¶](#section-7.3.4-7.4.1){.pilcrow}

    If the SEARCH results in no matches, the server [MUST NOT]{.bcp14}
    include the MAX return item in the ESEARCH response; however, it
    still [MUST]{.bcp14} send the ESEARCH
    response.[¶](#section-7.3.4-7.4.2){.pilcrow}

:   

ALL

:   []{#iref-all-search-return-item-name .iref} Returns all message
    numbers/UIDs that satisfy the SEARCH criteria using the sequence-set
    syntax. Each set [MUST]{.bcp14} be complete; in particular, a UID
    set is returned in an ESEARCH response only when each number in the
    range corresponds to an existing (matching) message. The client
    [MUST NOT]{.bcp14} assume that messages/UIDs will be listed in any
    particular order.[¶](#section-7.3.4-7.6.1){.pilcrow}

    If the SEARCH results in no matches, the server [MUST NOT]{.bcp14}
    include the ALL return item in the ESEARCH response; however, it
    still [MUST]{.bcp14} send the ESEARCH
    response.[¶](#section-7.3.4-7.6.2){.pilcrow}

:   

COUNT
:   []{#iref-count-search-return-item-na .iref} Returns the number of
    messages that satisfy the SEARCH criteria. This return item
    [MUST]{.bcp14} always be included in the ESEARCH
    response.[¶](#section-7.3.4-7.8){.pilcrow}
:   

Example:[¶](#section-7.3.4-8){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.3.4-9}
``` sourcecode
  S: * ESEARCH UID COUNT 17 ALL 4:18,21,28
```

[¶](#section-7.3.4-9){.pilcrow}
:::

Example:[¶](#section-7.3.4-10){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.3.4-11}
``` sourcecode
  S: * ESEARCH (TAG "a567") UID COUNT 17 ALL 4:18,21,28
```

[¶](#section-7.3.4-11){.pilcrow}
:::

Example:[¶](#section-7.3.4-12){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.3.4-13}
``` sourcecode
  S: * ESEARCH COUNT 18 ALL 1:17,21
```

[¶](#section-7.3.4-13){.pilcrow}
:::
:::

::: {#flags-resp}
::: {#section-7.3.5 .section}
#### [7.3.5.](#section-7.3.5){.section-number .selfRef} [FLAGS Response](#name-flags-response){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-flags-response}

[]{#iref-flags-response-193 .iref} []{.break}

Contents:
:   flag parenthesized list[¶](#section-7.3.5-1.2){.pilcrow}
:   

The FLAGS response occurs as a result of a SELECT or EXAMINE command.
The flag parenthesized list identifies the flags (at a minimum, the
system-defined flags) that are applicable for this mailbox. Flags other
than the system flags can also exist, depending on server
implementation.[¶](#section-7.3.5-2){.pilcrow}

The update from the FLAGS response [MUST]{.bcp14} be remembered by the
client.[¶](#section-7.3.5-3){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-7.3.5-4){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.3.5-5}
``` sourcecode
  S: * FLAGS (\Answered \Flagged \Deleted \Seen \Draft)
```

[¶](#section-7.3.5-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#section-7.4 .section}
### [7.4.](#section-7.4){.section-number .selfRef} [Server Responses - Mailbox Size](#name-server-responses-mailbox-si){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-server-responses-mailbox-si}

These responses are always untagged. This is how changes in the size of
the mailbox are transmitted from the server to the client. Immediately
following the \"\*\" token is a number that represents a message
count.[¶](#section-7.4-1){.pilcrow}

::: {#exists}
::: {#section-7.4.1 .section}
#### [7.4.1.](#section-7.4.1){.section-number .selfRef} [EXISTS Response](#name-exists-response){.section-name .selfRef} {#name-exists-response}

[]{.break}

Contents:
:   none[¶](#section-7.4.1-1.2){.pilcrow}
:   

The EXISTS response reports the number of messages in the mailbox. This
response occurs as a result of a SELECT or EXAMINE command and if the
size of the mailbox changes (e.g., new
messages).[¶](#section-7.4.1-2){.pilcrow}

The update from the EXISTS response [MUST]{.bcp14} be remembered by the
client.[¶](#section-7.4.1-3){.pilcrow}

Example:[¶](#section-7.4.1-4){.pilcrow}

::: {#section-7.4.1-5}
``` sourcecode
  S: * 23 EXISTS
```

[¶](#section-7.4.1-5){.pilcrow}
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#section-7.5 .section}
